U.S. Debt and the Greece Analogy
U.S. Debt and the Greece Analogy
By Alan Greenspan
1,301 words
18 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
An urgency to rein in budget deficits seems to be gaining some traction among American lawmakers. If so, it
is none too soon. Perceptions of a large U.S. borrowing capacity are misleading.
Despite the surge in federal debt to the public during the past 18 months -- to $8.6 trillion from $5.5 trillion --
inflation and long-term interest rates, the typical symptoms of fiscal excess, have remained remarkably
subdued. This is regrettable, because it is fostering a sense of complacency that can have dire
consequences.
The roots of the apparent debt market calm are clear enough. The financial crisis, triggered by the
unexpected default of Lehman Brothers in September 2008, created a collapse in global demand that
engendered a high degree of deflationary slack in our economy. The very large contraction of private
financing demand freed private saving to finance the explosion of federal debt. Although our financial
institutions have recovered perceptibly and returned to a degree of solvency, banks, pending a significant
increase in capital, remain reluctant to lend.
Beneath the calm, there are market signals that do not bode well for the future. For generations there had
been a large buffer between the borrowing capacity of the U.S. government and the level of its debt to the
public. But in the aftermath of the Lehman Brothers collapse, that gap began to narrow rapidly. Federal debt
to the public rose to 59% of GDP by mid-June 2010 from 38% in September 2008. How much borrowing
leeway at current interest rates remains for U.S. Treasury financing is highly uncertain.
The U.S. government can create dollars at will to meet any obligation, and it will doubtless continue to do so.
U.S. Treasurys are thus free of credit risk. But they are not free of interest rate risk. If Treasury net debt
issuance were to double overnight, for example, newly issued Treasury securities would continue free of
credit risk, but the Treasury would have to pay much higher interest rates to market its newly issued
securities.
In the wake of recent massive budget deficits, the difference between the 10-year swap rate and 10-year
Treasury note yield (the swap spread) declined to an unprecedented negative 13 basis points this March from
a positive 77 basis points in September 2008. This indicated that investors were requiring the U.S. Treasury
to pay an interest rate higher than rates that prevailed on comparable maturity private swaps.
(A private swap rate is the fixed interest rate required of a private bank or corporation to be exchanged for a
series of cash flow payments, based on floating interest rates, for a particular length of time. A dollar swap
spread is the swap rate less the interest rate on U.S. Treasury debt of the same maturity.)
At the height of budget surplus euphoria in 2000, the Office of Management and Budget, the Congressional
Budget Office and the Federal Reserve foresaw an elimination of marketable federal debt securities
outstanding. The 10-year swap spread in August 2000 reached a record 130 basis points. As the projected
surplus disappeared and deficits mounted, the 10-year swap spread progressively declined, turning negative
this March, and continued to deteriorate until the unexpected euro-zone crisis granted a reprieve to the U.S.
The 10-year swap spread quickly regained positive territory and by June 14 stood at a plus 12 basis points.
The sharp decline in the euro-dollar exchange rate since March reflects a large, but temporary, swing in the
intermediate demand for U.S. Treasury securities at the expense of euro issues.
The 10-year swap spread understandably has emerged as a sensitive proxy of Treasury borrowing capacity:
a so-called canary in the coal mine.
I grant that low long-term interest rates could continue for months, or even well into next year. But just as
easily, long-term rate increases can emerge with unexpected suddenness. Between early October 1979 and
late February 1980, for example, the yield on the 10-year note rose almost four percentage points.

--- Page 44 ---
Page 44 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
In the 1950s, as I remember them, U.S. federal budget deficits were no more politically acceptable than
households spending beyond their means. Regrettably, that now quaint notion gave way over the decades,
such that today it is the rare politician who doesn't run on seemingly costless spending increases or tax cuts
with borrowed money. A low tax burden is essential to maintain America's global competitiveness. But tax
cuts need to be funded by permanent outlay reductions.
The current federal debt explosion is being driven by an inability to stem new spending initiatives. Having
appropriated hundreds of billions of dollars on new programs in the last year and a half, it is very difficult for
Congress to deny an additional one or two billion dollars for programs that significant constituencies perceive
as urgent. The federal government is currently saddled with commitments for the next three decades that it
will be unable to meet in real terms. This is not new. For at least a quarter century analysts have been aware
of the pending surge in baby boomer retirees.
We cannot grow out of these fiscal pressures. The modest-sized post-baby-boom labor force, if history is any
guide, will not be able to consistently increase output per hour by more than 3% annually. The product of a
slowly growing labor force and limited productivity growth will not provide the real resources necessary to
meet existing commitments. (We must avoid persistent borrowing from abroad. We cannot count on
foreigners to finance our current account deficit indefinitely.)
Only politically toxic cuts or rationing of medical care, a marked rise in the eligible age for health and
retirement benefits, or significant inflation, can close the deficit. I rule out large tax increases that would sap
economic growth (and the tax base) and accordingly achieve little added revenues.
With huge deficits currently having no evident effect on either inflation or long-term interest rates, the
budget constraints of the past are missing. It is little comfort that the dollar is still the least worst of the major
fiat currencies. But the inexorable rise in the price of gold indicates a large number of investors are seeking a
safe haven beyond fiat currencies.
The United States, and most of the rest of the developed world, is in need of a tectonic shift in fiscal policy.
Incremental change will not be adequate. In the past decade the U.S. has been unable to cut any federal
spending programs of significance.
I believe the fears of budget contraction inducing a renewed decline of economic activity are misplaced. The
current spending momentum is so pressing that it is highly unlikely that any politically feasible fiscal constraint
will unleash new deflationary forces. I do not believe that our lawmakers or others are aware of the degree of
impairment of our fiscal brakes. If we contained the amount of issuance of Treasury securities, pressures on
private capital markets would be eased.
Fortunately, the very severity of the pending crisis and growing analogies to Greece set the stage for a
serious response. That response needs to recognize that the range of error of long-term U.S. budget
forecasts (especially of Medicare) is, in historic perspective, exceptionally wide. Our economy cannot afford a
major mistake in underestimating the corrosive momentum of this fiscal crisis. Our policy focus must therefore
err significantly on the side of restraint.
---
Mr. Greenspan, former chairman of the Federal Reserve, is president of Greenspan Associates LLC and
author of "The Age of Turbulence: Adventures in a New World" (Penguin, 2007).
(See related letter: "Letters to the Editor: Where Was Greenspan Back Then?" -- WSJ June 26, 2010)

Page 45 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Who's Minding the CEO? --- An Assistant Knows Her Boss Like Few Others; Why Their Partnership Is Imperiled
Who's Minding the CEO? --- An Assistant Knows Her Boss Like Few Others; Why Their Partnership Is
Imperiled
By Katherine Rosman
1,704 words
17 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
D1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
"It's like an office marriage," says Rosanne Badowski, 53, of working since 1988 as the executive assistant to
Jack Welch, the retired General Electric Co. chairman and CEO.
Mr. Welch disagrees. "This is smoother," he says.
Such long-term partnerships between an executive and an executive assistant (or "EA," "admin," or
"secretary," depending on your location and generation) are becoming increasingly rare. Executives change
companies more frequently, and while some assistants will relocate, many can't uproot to follow the boss.
Cost cutting and technology mean that fewer mid-level executives are assigned assistants, cutting back
chances to move up the ladder together. At some law firms in Manhattan, for example, as many as five
attorneys might share an assistant. At certain technology companies, having an assistant is considered
outdated.
"An assistant can buffer you from a lot," says Tim Westergren, the founder of Pandora, the Internet radio
company. For this reason, he is assistant-free.
Yet, at the same time, the demand for highly skilled assistants has increased in the last several years as
shareholders and customers demand greater connectedness with executives. Tech-savvy assistants
increasingly are targets of poaching by other executives. "The term chief of staff is being used a lot more,"
says David Goldman, the owner of Ruby Peak Recruiting, a San Francisco head-hunting firm which
specializes in placing assistants with top-level executives in the technology and venture capital sectors.
"Demonstrated longevity" is now an explicitly sought-after qualification, headhunters say. Sun Microsystem's
longtime CEO, Jonathan Schwartz, worked with his "admin" for 10 years before Oracle acquired Sun and he
left the company. Richard Parsons, chairman of the board of Citigroup Inc., has worked with his assistant for
22 years. The former Bear Stearns chairman Alan "Ace" Greenberg has three secretaries whose tenures with
him range from 10 to 25 years. When Jan Verhage moved from Washington, D.C., to New York this spring to
become chief operating officer of Girl Scouts of the USA, her last act at work was a sit-down conversation
with her assistant of 16 years. "It was a heart-string situation," Ms. Verhage says.
"It's about chemistry and trust, and when you have it, it's the difference between having an assistant who
manages your errands and a partner who is looking out for you and your career," says Flor De Leon, 36, who
worked for a decade as the assistant to Vish Makhijani, a tech-industry executive.
An assistant is involved in the nitty-gritty of a company's operations. She (and she is often though not always
a she) may listen to her boss' voicemail messages, if she's not listening in on calls directly. She is privy to
email correspondence and proprietary information. At some companies, assistants sign nondisclosure
agreements. Others rely on honor and discretion.
Dawn James worked for seven years as the assistant to Alfred Chuang, a founder and CEO of BEA Systems,
until the Silicon Valley company was acquired by Oracle in 2008. "They're getting pinged by 5 million people
a day. You have to be able to weed through the madness," says Ms. James. "I was a sponge absorbing the
business."
Many assistants to top executives say they know each other, only over email and the phone, from years of
connecting their bosses. They tap this network for tips on where best to pull together last-minute board
meetings, find a limousine service in Shanghai, and to vent about bosses who take out their frustrations on
their aides.
Ms. Badowski was working for a GE senior vice president in 1988 and applying for sales jobs within the
company when human resources encouraged her to apply for the job managing the life of Mr. Welch, a CEO
with 350,000 employees. She ran Mr. Welch's GE office for 13 years. After he retired from the company in

--- Page 46 ---
Page 46 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
2001 and eventually moved to Boston and remarried, Ms. Badowski moved too, even though she had never
lived outside Connecticut. Then the Welches moved to Palm Beach Gardens, Fla., a year ago, so too did Ms.
Badowski.
Ms. Badowski tells Mr. Welch her opinion, even when she knows he's not going to agree. And she will defy
his orders on occasion. "I'm not going to do something inefficiently just because he's told me to," she says.
"It's for his own good."
When Mr. Welch launched the Jack Welch Management Institute, an online MBA program through Chancellor
University, this year, he had Ms. Badowski enroll as a member of its first class. She is giving Mr. Welch an
insider's analysis. Ms. Badowski has not hidden from her instructors her connection to their boss, nor has she
advertised it.
"I respect her views of what is working and what isn't," Mr. Welch says. "Candor is what you want. As an
executive, you search for that."
High-level assistants are well compensated. When an executive changes jobs or is promoted, the assistant's
pay is often negotiated by the boss as a part of his or her overall deal. Of the placements Ruby Peak has
made in 2010 in San Francisco's Bay Area, the average starting salary has been $72,000, a base which
excludes bonuses, profit-sharing and 401(k) plans. Working as the assistant to a "screamer" might entail a
base salary of $100,000, Mr. Goldman says.
In movies, plays and television shows, secretaries have often been portrayed as temptations for married
executives. While surely some professional partnerships have morphed into personal relationships, assistants
to top-tier executives say having trust and frequent communication with their boss' family is vital. "You need
to partner with your exec's spouse," says Melanie Delaney, an administrative assistant. "You don't want to
send them to school for a school play when there is no school play."
For a decade, she was the "admin" to Mr. Schwartz, then the CEO of Sun Microsystems, Ms. Delaney, 38,
also helped him to have a life: She blocked out time for him to work out several times a week. She was kept
apprised of his kids' activities by his wife and made sure he attended as many as possible. She looked after
his health too -- making sure he took time to eat and that what he was eating was in keeping with the
appropriate regimen. "If they're doing no carbs, there can be no carbs," she says. "I put thought into what I
fed my guy."
After the company was acquired by Oracle in January, both Ms. Delaney and Mr. Schwartz left. She now
works for the president of a pharmaceutical company. He is deciding what to do next.
"Melanie understood all my priorities and not simply the Sun priorities," Mr. Schwartz says. "She was critical
to my productivity. She was my liaison to customers, shareholders, to the media, to my employee base, to
members of my family. She was at the nexus of all of that." When he left Sun, he didn't think he would have
enough work to justify keeping Ms. Delaney employed.
The first day, he triple-booked lunch. "Then I failed to show up for all three," he says.
---
Scenes With The Secretary
In movies, television, plays and books about the workplace, the trusted sidekick and confidant is often the
secretary. The dramatized assistant tends to fall into one of several archetypes.
Amorous
Administration can turn to ardor. In the 2008 movie "Iron Man," Gwyneth Paltrow's Pepper Potts falls for the
boss. On "Arrested Development," the TV show, George Bluth Sr., played by Jeffrey Tambor, had an affair
with Kitty, played by Judy Greer, his partner in crime among other activities. In the 2009 hit film, "The
Proposal," Ryan Reynolds's character hates his boss, played by Sandra Bullock, through most of the film, but
then he reconsiders, and they wed. In 2002's "Secretary," Maggie Gyllenhaal abandons an unusual working
relationship with her boss for a domesticated personal one.
Ambitious
Some fictional assistants can't hide their desire to move up. Peggy Olson of "Mad Men" goes from Don
Draper's secretary to copywriter. In the 1988 movie "Working Girl," Melanie Griffith as Tess McGill wears her
ambition as prominently as her teased outer-borough hair. Initially, Tess's hopes are quashed by her boss,
played by Sigourney Weaver. But by movie's end, she meets her very own secretary.
Downtrodden

--- Page 47 ---
Page 47 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Screamers, on screen and off, unleash on their assistants. On HBO's "Entourage," Lloyd, played by Rex Lee,
steals the scene as the long-suffering assistant to hot-headed Ari Gold. In the 1994 movie "Swimming with
Sharks," Kevin Spacey is a nasty film-industry executive whose emotionally abused assistant snaps --
kidnapping and torturing his boss. In hit movie based on the book "The Devil Wears Prada," Andy Sachs,
played by Anne Hathaway, has the innocence (and dowdiness) beaten out of her by the terror-inducing
fashion editor played by Meryl Streep.
Brains
Sometimes, she's the brain behind the man. On "Mad Men," Joan Harris, played by Christina Hendricks, is
office manager and the Alpha-secretary. Lily Tomlin's Violet Newstead from 1980's "9-to-5" does her boss's
job and watches him take credit. Jane Hathaway saves Mr. Drysdale from his own greed and stupidity on
"The Beverly Hillbillies." On recently canceled ABC show, "Ugly Betty," the title character basically runs the
magazine for the editor in chief she is employed to assist.
Bombshells
The beautiful assistant is probably one of the most over-used stereotypes, and the most galling to
professional assistants. In the 2001 musical, "The Producers," secretary Ulla isn't talking about her
stenography skills when she sings "When You Got It, Flaunt It." Uma Thurman played Ulla in the 2005 film. In
"9-to-5," Dolly Parton's Doralee Rhodes was assumed wrongly by the office to be the boss's mistress. In the
James Bond series, Miss Moneypenny is the assistant to the head of the British Secret Service but she flirts
with 007.

Wealth Adviser (A Special Report) --- Other People's Money: A guide to getting started in buying and selling foreign currencies -- either on...
Wealth Adviser (A Special Report) --- Other People's Money: A guide to getting started in buying and
selling foreign currencies -- either on your own or with the help of a professional
By Jonathan Burton
1,493 words
14 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R7
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
What's in your wallet? Probably not euros or pounds or yen. Yet U.S. investors who may not know rupees
from ringgits are buying and selling foreign currencies both as a hedge against their U.S. dollar holdings and
as a way to speculate on global economic upheaval.
A lot of the action is in mutual funds, exchange-traded funds and exchange-traded notes that focus on a
single currency or a basket of currencies. Some of these investments also let shareholders sell short in order
to profit from a currency's decline. And some experienced traders deal with foreign-exchange brokers who
allow buying and selling with leverage -- money borrowed from the broker -- which magnifies gains, but also
compounds losses.
U.S. investors are affected by currency fluctuations even if they don't trade in foreign money directly. When
the dollar is weak, gains from international stocks and bonds are worth more in dollars and boost U.S.
investors' returns. Similarly, when the greenback strengthens, gains can be worth less. The same forces
apply to the foreign-based earnings of U.S.-based blue-chips in the Standard &Poor's 500-stock index, which
collectively ring up almost half of their sales in other countries.
Thus, buying foreign currencies themselves can be a hedge in turbulent times, because they bear almost no
relation to the movement of stocks, bonds and other traditional investments. Owning foreign currencies also
can help allay concerns that U.S. government spending will spur inflation and erode the purchasing power of
the dollar.
Big swings can occur in currency values, too. The euro has lost more than 15% of its value against the dollar
so far this year as the sovereign debt crisis pummels Europe's common currency. Still, for investors whose
wealth is largely tied up in U.S. dollars, says Roger Nusbaum, a financial adviser in Prescott, Ariz., "putting
some money into a foreign currency or any sort of short-term foreign debt is really a means of hiding some
money away from what looks like fundamentally a lousy currency, that being the U.S. dollar."
"The U.S. dollar is the least ugly in the ugly contest," adds Mr. Nusbaum, who favors the commodity-based
currencies of Australia, Brazil, Chile and Canada, along with the robust economies of Singapore, Denmark
and Norway.
"Commodity-based economies tend to be at different points in their economic cycles, so by extension can be
at different points in their stock-market cycles, which affects the currencies," Mr. Nusbaum says. "It gives you
a much better chance of real diversification, a real zig-zag effect, versus owning Europe, which is far more
similar to the U.S."
Individual investors can start a forex account with just a few hundred dollars. Trading well requires close
monitoring of exchange rates, statements by central bankers and other economic developments. Generally,
currencies rise in value when countries raise their interest rates and weaken when rates fall.
But it's easy to get blindsided. Only a year ago people were sure the dollar was dead and the euro was king.
Now the euro is at its lowest level in years.
Consider the tax implications as well: Profits from currency-based ETFs and ETNs are taxed as ordinary
income, and so typically at a higher rate than capital gains.
For those who decide to invest in currencies and are comfortable making their own judgments about currency
strengths and countries' fundamentals, a forex trading account or foreign-currency certificates of deposit are
good options.
To tap this market with professional help, meanwhile, there are lots of actively managed funds and indexed
offerings.

--- Page 49 ---
Page 49 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
When choosing a mutual fund, "Employ a manager who understands the asset class well and has some
historical performance," says Paul Justice, an analyst at investment researcher Morningstar Inc.
One currency specialist is Merk Investments, whose founder, Axel Merk, runs Merk Hard Currency, Merk
Absolute Return Currency, and Merk Asian Currency.
The Hard Currency fund looks to profit from a decline in the U.S. dollar through a basket of money-market
instruments tied to the euro, Swiss franc, Norwegian krone, Australian dollar, New Zealand dollar, Canadian
dollar, and gold.
Absolute Return, instead of basing its strategy on the direction of the dollar, can go long or short on various
world currencies, including the dollar.
Another offering that uses foreign money markets to bet against the U.S. dollar is Franklin Templeton Hard
Currency. The fund tends to shine when the dollar is weak and lag in periods of dollar strength.
Many funds have complex strategies that use futures and swaps to gain currency exposure. In contrast,
CurrencyShares ETFs from Rydex Investments directly hold major currencies. Unlike most ETFs,
CurrencyShares ETFs also don't track an index. They're set up as trusts that reflect the price of the
underlying currency. These include the CurrencyShares Euro Trust, British Pound Sterling Trust, Japanese
Yen Trust, Swiss Franc Trust, Swedish Krona Trust and Canadian Dollar Trust. Emerging-markets exposure
is available, too, with the Mexican Peso Trust and Russian Ruble Trust. In each of these ETFs, the
shareholders make money if the currency being held appreciates against the U.S. dollar. They lose money if
the dollar strengthens.
EverBank Financial Corp. is a seller of foreign-currency certificates of deposit. Its WorldCurrency CDs come
in both single currencies and baskets of currencies. These CDs are available in terms of three to 12 months.
Keep in mind that currencies of countries with fast-growing or commodity-based economies tend to yield
more than those of countries with tepid growth. But higher yield generally means greater risk.
EverBank's basket-based CDs feature currencies grouped by geography and macro-economic themes, such
as energy and central banks that show fiscal discipline. These come in three- or six-month terms.
One reason to go with a basket: A blend of currencies is less volatile than a single currency. But as with any
bank CD, you're locking up money for an extended time and are penalized for early withdrawal.
There are dozens of developed- and emerging-market ETFs based on currencies. WisdomTree, an ETF
provider, in conjunction with Dreyfus Funds, offers exposure to emerging markets with WisdomTree Dreyfus
Chinese Yuan, Brazilian Real, South African Rand and Indian rupee funds. WisdomTree Dreyfus Emerging
Currency is a smorgasbord of the Chilean peso, Turkish new lira, Chinese yuan, Israeli shekel and others.
PowerShares DB G10 Currency Harvest is an ETF that uses a long-short strategy to capitalize on the
so-called carry trade, where an investor borrows in the currency of a country with low interest rates and
invests in a country with higher interest rates. The difference is known as the carry.
For something more simple, there are also funds that make straightforward plays on the direction of the U.S.
dollar. PowerShares offers US Dollar Bullish and US Dollar Bearish funds. ProFunds lays out similar
objectives with its unleveraged Rising US Dollar Investor and Falling US Dollar Investor.
Various ETNs that invest in currencies are available as well. ETNs are like ETFs, in that they are traded like
stocks and bonds. But in essence they are bond-like promises to pay, though the debt is unsecured, and their
value hinges on the issuer's credit rating. Offerings in this niche include Barclays iPath EUR/USD Exchange
Rate, tied to the euro, iPath JPY/USD Exchange Rate, tied to the Japanese yen, and iPath GBP/USD
Exchange Rate, tied to the pound. Others include Morgan Stanley Market Vectors funds linked to the rupee,
Market Vectors Rupee/USD and China's yuan, Market Vectors Renminbi/USD.
Going up the risk scale, Rydex offers its Strengthening Dollar 2x Strategy, which seeks to deliver 200% of the
daily performance of the U.S. Dollar Index, a gauge of the dollar's value in relation to a basket of other
currencies. The fund uses instruments such as index swaps, futures and options. And for days when the
dollar weakens, Rydex has another fund, Weakening Dollar 2x Strategy, which seeks to provide 200% of the
inverse of the decline. The fund uses short-selling and derivatives similar to those used by the Strengthening
fund.
ProFunds tries to match the daily performance of the Dollar Index, before fees and expenses, with its Rising
US Dollar Investor and Falling US Dollar Investor funds. For leveraged currency investing ETFs, the firm also
features the double-leveraged ProShares Ultra Euro, and ProShares Ultra Yen.

--- Page 50 ---
Page 50 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Currency funds can be volatile enough without leverage, so these juiced investments are really only suitable
for active traders, not long-term investors.

Good as Gold May Not Be Good Enough for Money
Letters to the Editor
Good as Gold May Not Be Good Enough for Money
784 words
14 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A16
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Rick Dickson's June 8 Letter "Let's Move Away From Fiat Money" exemplifies the widespread misconception
that replacing fiat money with a gold standard would stabilize the value of money and restore confidence in
the dollar. That misperception leads to the dangerous conclusion that we would no longer need the Federal
Reserve Board of Governors managing the money supply.
In fact, the only true foundation for a stable currency is a strong economy, for which money merely provides
lubrication. If money-supply growth is synchronized with the real economy's needs, the virtuous result is the
stable currency that Adam Smith told us is one of the responsibilities of government. Too much or too little
money results in unacceptable inflation or deflation -- both of which have a lengthy world-wide track record
of causing not just economic turmoil, but also political problems, up to and including the overthrow of
governments.
I reject the idea of a gold-backed dollar for two reasons. First, the gold supply cannot be controlled as readily
as the gold bugs like to imagine. For example, picture what would happen to the so-called intrinsic value of
gold in the wake of a technology breakthrough enabling inexpensive extraction of gold from seawater. The
result would be hyperinflation, because nobody would put gold back into the ground or dump it back into the
sea in order to keep the money supply from skyrocketing. Poof -- so much for the "intrinsic" value of gold.
Second, the amount of control we humans do have over the gold supply rests primarily in foreign hands.
Expecting the Russians, South Africans and Indonesians to modulate the gold supply as necessary to
support a stable U.S. dollar is about as naive as it gets.
Consequently, I support the fiat dollar, backed by our economy and managed by the Fed Board of Governors.
The supply of fiat dollars (base money plus credit money) is more directly controllable than the gold supply,
and is the responsibility of duly appointed Americans, albeit imperfect, whom we can hold accountable for
maintaining a stable dollar.
Is Ben Bernanke perfect? No, but I'd rather keep his Fed in control of the dollar's value than to yield control to
Russian gold-mine owners.
A strong, growing economy is the only thing that backs our dollar. Enabling it is the responsibility of our
political leaders -- not the Fed, not the gold miners. But that's a different subject.
Steve Conover
McKinney, Texas
---
Contrary to Seth Lipsky's "'Pieces of Eight': The Constitution and the Dollar" (op-ed, May 29), or Rick
Dickson's letter, that fiat money is at the heart of our financial mess, the real culprit is "artificial money."
Exploding exponentially, the monetary value of all private contracts (which is at the heart of investment
banking), including the likes of credit default swaps and other derivative financial instruments, now dwarfs any
government's ability to track it, let alone provide a basis on which to rationally manage or regulate it. The
credit-rating agencies are proof of that.
When making money from artificial transactions becomes the business model rather than the more
fundamental business model of producing goods and services, then we are all in for a rough economic road.
What rational business model incorporates risk in its interest rate and then, instead of spreading the risk
through reinsurance, goes out and creates an artificial financial instrument compounding that risk at full face
value as a bet against its own investment or loan? And then, because there is so much money floating
around in our economy from our economic successes, what model creates other derivative "investments" on
top of that? If we believe such highly leveraged "churning" (or outright gambling as we have seen over the

--- Page 52 ---
Page 52 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
past 10 years) has some socially redeeming value other than pure speculation to generate fees and profits for
our "bankers" (be they commercial or investment), then my view is simply to tax such
non-value-added-transactions at very high marginal rates -- the 90% from the 1950s sounds about right.
There will never be enough precious metal to correspond to the increasing value of all goods and services
being produced and used in our growing world economy to meet some arbitrary ratio. So let's get it together
and focus on more relevant issues, like what value artificial money transactions have in a society that really
needs to build long-term capital for retirement and health care? And that doesn't mean reverting to the
meaningless tunnel-vision debate about paper or gold or silver.
John O. King
Stafford, Va.

World News: Coordinated Attack Kills Dozens in Baghdad

461 words
14 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BAGHDAD -- Insurgents wearing military uniforms stormed Iraq's central bank Sunday during an apparent
robbery attempt, battling security forces in a three-hour standoff after bombs exploded nearby in a
coordinated daylight attack that left as many as 26 people dead.
The assault stoked fears that insurgents are taking advantage of the political deadlock after inconclusive
March 7 national elections to try to derail security gains. The 325-member parliament was to convene
Monday, but agreement on a new government could still be months away, analysts say.
Iraqi military spokesman Maj. Gen. Qassim al-Moussawi blamed the attack on al Qaeda in Iraq, but said no
money had been stolen from the bank, which holds gold deposits as well as U.S. and Iraqi currency.
The violence began with the daylight bombings, which sent plumes of smoke over the city skyline. There
were conflicting reports about the number and nature of the blasts.
The first bomb went off on the road near an electrical generator, Gen. al-Moussawi said. Insurgents wearing
army uniforms then tried to enter the bank through two entrances, exchanging gunfire with the guards. Three
suicide bombers detonated explosive vests at the main entrance of the bank, the general said, while two
other militants were killed by security forces at the second gate. Iraqi security forces then stormed the
building, prompting a standoff.
A number of attackers managed to get to a higher floor and set a fire to documents, the general said. The
motive appeared to be to steal the bank's deposits, then blow up the building.
Local police said a bomb in a parked car also exploded about 900 yards from the bank.
Violent robberies that bear some of the hallmarks of politically motivated attacks have been on the rise in
Iraq, as sectarian violence ebbs. Iraqi officials have attributed some of them to cash-strapped militants
desperately trying to raise money for their operations.
Sunday's attack appeared also to have a political motive as insurgents led by al Qaeda in Iraq seek to
undermine confidence in the U.S.-backed government and other state institutions.
The narrow victory of the Sunni-backed Iraqiya party in the March 7 vote was initially heralded as a
groundbreaking step toward a secular government after years of Sunni-Shiite tensions. But months of political
maneuvering have left the government in limbo.
Meanwhile, gunmen killed two policemen in the northern city of Mosul, which has been one of the hardest
areas to tame since the 2003 U.S.-led invasion, Iraqi officials said.
Also Sunday, an independent watchdog said Iraq's government suffered more corruption last year than any
other since the U.S.-led invasion in 2003.

Wealth Adviser (A Special Report) --- Alternative Investing: Buying Bonds of Low-Debt Nations, And How to Bet Against the Dollar
Wealth Adviser (A Special Report) --- Alternative Investing: Buying Bonds of Low-Debt Nations, And
How to Bet Against the Dollar
By Gregory Zuckerman
918 words
14 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Q: How can an investor buy government and corporate bonds from Norway, Australia, New Zealand and
Brazil?
A: Investors have been dumping the bonds and stocks of nations with heavy debts and limp growth
prospects, such as Greece, Spain, Italy and Portugal. Rather than stick with safe but low-yielding investments
such as U.S. money-market accounts, some experts recommend bonds from nations with limited debt and
robust growth outlooks.
The oil-rich nation of Norway, for example, has a less-than-4% chance of defaulting on its debt by 2015,
according to recent prices of credit-default swap contracts that serve as insurance on that debt. The chance
of default for Greece, by contrast, is seen as 75%. Australia, New Zealand and Brazil also are seen as safe
bets.
An investor who buys an individual bond and holds it to maturity can enjoy interest payments without worrying
about how it trades day-to-day.
Any weakness in these nations' currencies can take a bite out of any bond profits, however, even if the
countries never miss a bond payment. And while most brokers will sell these bonds to customers, many
experts say purchasing the debt in amounts of less than $100,000 can elicit quotes that are well off the
market price, hurting any chance for profit, says Sean Figueroa, co-founder of Cypress Wealth Advisors LLC
in San Francisco.
"The best method to gain access to these specific markets is either through exchange-traded funds, mutual
funds or closed-end mutual funds," says William Larkin, portfolio manager of fixed income at Salem,
Mass.-based Cabot Money Management Inc.
Unfortunately, there aren't many ways to bet on the currencies of some nations, like Norway.
"The Norwegian krone is one of the hardest to bet with because there is no ETF based on the currency or on
the Norwegian market," says J. Scott Miller Jr. of Blue Bell Private Wealth Management LLC in Blue Bell, Pa.
One option: the Global X FTSE Nordic 30 ETF (GXF). About 20% of its assets are invested in Norwegian
shares.
Mr. Larkin is a fan of Templeton Global Income Fund (GIM), which holds mostly foreign-government debt,
including about 8% in Brazilian debt; it yields more than 5.5%. He also recommends the ING Global Bond
Fund (IGBIX), a global bond fund with a 10% exposure to Brazil that yields about 6.5%, and the Aberdeen
Global Income Fund (FCO), a bond fund with 15% exposure to Australia and 7% to New Zealand. It yields
about 7.6%.
For those with an interest in New Zealand, Kevin Mahn, chief investment officer at Hennion &Walsh Asset
Management Inc. in Parsippany, N.J., recommends the WisdomTree Dreyfus New Zealand Dollar Fund
(BNZ), which tracks money-market rates in New Zealand and moves in the New Zealand dollar. Many
economists expect interest rates in New Zealand to rise as the economy strengthens. Mr. Mahn also is a fan
of WisdomTree Dreyfus Brazilian Real Fund (BZF), which tracks short-term securities in Brazil as well as
moves in the Brazilian real.
Q: Is there a better way to bet against the dollar than gold?
A: Some investors have been buying gold-related investments to protect against weakness in leading global
currencies. The move has worked in recent months, amid a rally for gold.

--- Page 55 ---
Page 55 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
But some are uncomfortable using gold as a hedge against possible future problems for the U.S. dollar. Gold
doesn't always move in an inverse direction to the dollar and already has climbed to the highest level in
years.
"So many other factors move the price of gold [that] the dollar could be dropping and gold could drop as
well," says Matthew Tuttle, president of Tuttle Wealth Management LLC, White Plains, N.Y.
At the same time, some contrarian investors argue that the U.S. dollar's rally versus many European nations
won't continue.
Some analysts recommend the PowerShares DB US Dollar Index Bearish (UDN), an ETF that represents a
short bet against the U.S. dollar as compared with the euro, Japanese yen, British pound, Canadian dollar,
Swedish krona and Swiss franc. But Blue Bell's Mr. Miller says the euro and pound represent the majority of
the holdings of this ETF.
"Most investors that I know that want to bet against the U.S. dollar also want to bet against the euro and even
the British pound," Mr. Miller says.
Mr. Miller recommends an emerging-markets currency ETF, such as the WisdomTree Dreyfus Emerging
Currency Fund (CEW). The fund is a wager on emerging-markets currencies, such as the Mexican peso,
Brazilian real, Chilean peso and Israeli shekel, relative to the U.S. dollar.
Another indirect way to hedge against a fall in the dollar: Buy oil and other commodities, which often rise
when paper currencies weaken. The IndexIQ ARB Global Resources ETF (GRES) owns shares in eight
commodity and resource categories, including energy, precious metals and timber.

Commodities Report
Oil Falls as Data Reinforce Demand Worries --- Report on Retail Sales Helps Quash a Rally That Had
Pushed Futures Above $75 a Barrel; Mixed Signals From China
By David Bird
612 words
12 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
B13
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
NEW YORK -- Crude-oil futures prices dropped as weak U.S. May retail-sales data stirred fresh worries about
the pace of economic recovery in the world's biggest oil consumer.
Retail sales tumbled 1.2%, in the first drop since September 2009, the Commerce Department said.
Economists surveyed by Dow Jones Newswires were expecting a 0.2% increase.
Light, sweet crude oil futures for July delivery on the New York Mercantile Exchange settled down $1.70 a
barrel, or 2.3%, at $73.78 a barrel.
Crude snapped a three-day rally that had lifted prices by a little more than $4 a barrel to a four-week high
above $75 a barrel on Thursday. Now the contract looks likely to challenge the low end of a $68-$78 a barrel
range.
"The disappointing retail sales refocused the market on economic conditions," said Gene McGillian, an
analyst at Tradition Energy in Stamford, Conn. "The market is really paying close attention to the economic
reports and is treating oil as an asset class," which is swayed more by financial signals than by oil-market
fundamentals of supply and demand, he said.
Still, U.S. oil-inventory data show continued high inventories and sluggish demand. Data released
Wednesday showed crude-oil stockpiles fell more than expected, as refiners lifted crude-oil processing to the
highest level since mid-July 2008. But the higher production kept gasoline inventories from dropping and
pushed stockpiles of diesel fuel and heating oil to the highest level since February. Demand for gasoline fell
by 1% from a year ago in the latest four-week period, heading into the peak summer driving season.
Expectations that crude-oil inventories at Cushing, Okla., the delivery point for the Nymex crude-oil contract,
will continue to rise to record highs near 40 million barrels added to concerns of a near-term oversupply. That
helped widen the discount of July-delivery crude futures to August futures to $1.56 a barrel, the most since
June 2. It compares with a $1.20 a barrel discount on Thursday.
"The retail [sales] number just adds more nervousness to the whole recovery effort," said Greg Lawson, risk
manager at Summit Energy in Louisville, Ky. "If the U.S. and Europe aren't spending, China and India need to
pick up the pace."
Traders said mixed signals from China, the second-largest oil consumer after the U.S., are being closely
watched, amid fears of growing inflation. In the near-term robust Chinese demand will keep oil prices from
slipping much below $70 a barrel, Mr. McGillian said. China said Friday crude-oil processing rates hit a record
high in May and were nearly 15% above a year earlier.
July delivery reformulated gasoline blendstock futures prices fell 1%, or 2.08 cents a gallon, to settle at
$2.0497 a gallon. July heating oil futures fell 1.4%, or 2.75 cents, to $2.0053.
In other commodity markets:
GOLD: Futures gained as investors tiptoed away from riskier assets after the surprise decline in retail sales
in May. Gold for June delivery settled $8.10, or 0.7%, higher at $1228.90 an ounce on the Comex division of
the Nymex.
COFFEE: U.S. arabica coffee prices surged 6%, taking cues from the London robusta contract, on a decline
in exchange-certified coffee-bean stockpiles. Coffee futures for July delivery settled 7.95 cents, or 6%, higher
at $1.4495 a pound on ICE Futures U.S.

U.S. News: Deflation Fears Stir in Developed Economies
U.S. News: Deflation Fears Stir in Developed Economies
By Jon Hilsenrath
862 words
11 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
A4
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Worries about consumer price deflation are resurfacing in the world's developed economies after weeks of
financial-market turmoil driven by Europe's fiscal crisis.
The fears are most pronounced in Europe, where policy makers are under pressure to reduce large budget
deficits now, before durable recoveries emerge. A combination of spending cuts and tax increases could
weigh on economic growth and feed into deflation, which is a broad decline in consumer prices.
Deflation makes it harder for consumers, businesses and governments to pay off debts. Principal repayments
on debt are fixed but deflation is marked by falling incomes, so as deflation sets in the burden of paying off
old debts gets greater.
Officials fret about deflation because it is hard to stop. Interest rates are already near zero in the U.S. and
elsewhere, so policy makers can't use the traditional tool of rate cuts to spur growth and stop deflation.
One leading indicator, Ireland, which has already experienced deflation amid severe fiscal austerity, reported
Thursday that consumer prices were down 1.1% from a year earlier in May, though the declines have
moderated in the past few months.
In the U.S. the threat looks more remote, but economists are beginning to warn that if the recovery falters, the
risk of a deflation bout will increase.
In one sign of rising alertness to the threat, yields on 10-year Treasury bonds -- which fall when inflation
worries recede and rise when inflation worries increase -- have dropped from nearly 4% in early April to
about 3.3%.
Though yields firmed a bit Thursday, big bond houses like Pacific Investment Management Co. have been
moving into the safe-haven instrument.
"There are clear warning signs of deflation," Anthony Sanders, a George Mason University professor, warned
at a Federal Reserve conference about housing in Cleveland Thursday. "My friends at the [Federal Reserve]
may not agree with me. If the banks don't lend, we will get deflation."
Policy makers in the U.S. and elsewhere have been worrying about deflation for much of the past decade.
The Fed's low-interest-rate policies of the early part of last decade were designed in part to avoid it. So far, it
hasn't taken hold in any large economy outside of Japan. And in developing economies, inflation driven by
overheated growth remains the primary concern -- officials in Brazil raised interest rates by 0.75 percentage
point Thursday to 10.25% to contain growth.
The Fed's own efforts in the past few months have been geared toward reassuring a skeptical public that
inflation, not deflation, wouldn't be the next threat after it pushed interest rates to near zero to combat the
recession. Inflation is still slowing sharply. U.S. consumer prices were up 2.2% in May from a year earlier
and could tick lower as food and gasoline prices fall. Excluding the volatile food and energy sectors,
consumer prices were up just 0.9% in May, the smallest increase since 1966.
The Fed is hoping that it has fought inflation to a standstill by pushing interest rates to near zero and
pumping more than $1 trillion into the financial system through loans and bond purchases. While all of that
money could in theory cause inflation, weak banks and the immense amount of slack in the economy are
weighing against that.
Subtle hints of wariness about deflation have crept into the talk of some officials. In a speech Wednesday,
Brian Sack, who runs the Federal Reserve Bank of New York's powerful markets group, said U.S. officials
needed to be as worried about the risks of inflation falling too much as they are about the threat that prices

--- Page 59 ---
Page 59 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
could soar. "I think the concerns about inflation should be two-sided," he said, meaning the upside and the
downside.
Federal Reserve Chairman Ben Bernanke noted Wednesday that even though gold prices have been
soaring -- a potential indicator of inflation fears -- many other inflation indicators are going the other way,
including yields on U.S. Treasury bonds and prices for commodities. The price of copper, for instance, is
down 22% since early April. The average price of regular unleaded gasoline in the U.S. is down 19 cents from
a month ago, according to AAA.
Fed officials are just as concerned about what people think inflation will do in the future as they are about
what it has already done. If people start to believe that inflation will rise or fall sharply, their behavior can
point actual inflation in the direction they're expecting.
At the Baltimore-based trucking firm Cowan Systems LLC, expectations are being pulled in two directions.
Joe Cowan, the firm's president, says the rates his company charges are 15% to 20% below where they were
in 2007.
"There's too many trucks chasing too little freight," Mr. Cowan says. But he thinks a turnaround could be
brewing because many small trucking companies have gone out of business and those that remain will be
able to negotiate higher rates.

Justin Lahart and David Wessel contributed to this article.

Unusual Pair: Investors Buy Gold but Also U.S. Bonds
Unusual Pair: Investors Buy Gold but Also U.S. Bonds
By Mark Gongloff
714 words
11 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors have piled into gold and Treasury bonds, seeking havens. History suggests only one of those bets
might end up paying off. Is this time different?
After their strong runs, both gold and Treasury debt are expensive -- suggesting the risk to investors is
growing and the potential for reward is shrinking.
While a seemingly incongruous pairing -- Treasurys and gold typically move in opposite directions depending
on investors' concerns about inflation or deflation -- the two have benefited from worries that the European
debt crisis is spreading. There has been talk that central banks have shed some European assets in favor of
gold and U.S. debt.
"We have a short-term risk aversion and a lack of suitable investment alternatives," said Howard Simons,
strategist at Bianco Research in Chicago. "The policy response of printing money whenever anything goes
wrong, telling investors everywhere that paper becomes worthless because they've printed too much -- that's
how you get the simultaneous reaction of bonds and gold rallying."
The two may not move together forever. In the event of a sudden bout of inflation, Treasurys could tumble,
and gold gain. But if inflation continues to slow or turns into deflation, it could be the opposite reaction.
Each makes a good hedge against the other. And each offers protection against further market turmoil.
Both gold and Treasurys retreated on Thursday as risk appetite crept back into financial markets and stock
prices rose.
But they both are still near recent milestones. Gold earlier this week surged to a Comex record of $1,244 an
ounce. Yields on 10-year Treasury notes, which fall as the price rises, have been hovering around their
lowest levels in a year. The only time in history that 10-year Treasury yields were lower was in the fall and
winter of 2008-09, at the worst of the financial crisis.
Even before this week, gold and Treasurys had enjoyed a decadelong rally together, while stock markets
have taken a bruising amid years of financial and geopolitical turmoil.
Treasurys have returned 102% since the start of 2000, according to data from Ryan Labs Asset
Management. Gold has soared 328%. Total returns on the S&P 500-stock index, meantime, have been
slightly negative.
Such numbers make some contrarian investors lick their chops, particularly with individual-investor money
piling into both assets. Investors have pumped $10 billion into Treasury bond funds this year, on the heels of
nearly $24 billion in 2009, according to Lipper FMI, a unit of Thomson Reuters.
Net inflows into SPDR Gold Shares exchange-traded fund totaled $4.35 billion in May -- the third-highest on
record after $5.6 billion and $4.6 billion in February and March 2009, respectively, according to State Street
Global Advisors.
The main risk for buyers of Treasurys is that a rebound in economic growth, combined with easy monetary
policy and large government budget deficits, will spark inflation.
During the runaway inflation of the 1970s, annual Treasury returns kept up with stock-market returns, in part
because bond yields were relatively high.
Yields are far lower today, suggesting there is less of a cushion for investors if interest rates jump. In 1994,
Treasury returns were negative, due mainly to an unexpected reversal in Federal Reserve monetary policy
that year. Before those rises, 10-year Treasurys yielded 5.8%.

--- Page 63 ---
Page 63 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"In my time in the fixed-income business, about 30 years, rarely has high-grade fixed income been this risky,"
said Brad Tank, chief investment officer for fixed income at Neuberger Berman. He sees better deals in
stocks, some corporate and mortgage bonds and possibly longer-dated Treasury debt.
Curtis Arledge, chief investment officer of fixed income, fundamental portfolios at money manager BlackRock
Inc. also has been buying longer-dated corporate and mortgage debt, citing higher yields relative to
Treasurys. However, he has a less-optimistic outlook for the economy given anemic levels of private
borrowing. Annualized total borrowing in the U.S. economy is at least $2 trillion lower than its average in the
past decade.
That suggests there is little room for interest rates to rise in the near future and keeps alive the risk of
deflation, both of which are boons to Treasurys.

Dow Rallies 273.28, Back Over 10000
Thursday's Markets
Dow Rallies 273.28, Back Over 10000
By Peter A. McKay
361 words
11 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors ignored recent jitters about global growth, oil spills and sovereign-debt defaults and made big bets

As Gold Hits Record, Central Banks in Focus --- Yellow Metal Settles at $1,244 as Indications Emerge of a Shift Out of the Euro and Into...
Commodities Report
As Gold Hits Record, Central Banks in Focus --- Yellow Metal Settles at $1,244 as Indications Emerge
of a Shift Out of the Euro and Into Safer Assets
By Carolyn Cui and Mark Gongloff
558 words
9 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C12
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold's surge to a record sparked speculation that central banks may be stepping up purchases of the
precious metal.
Tuesday, gold contracts for June delivery rose $4.70, or 0.4%, to $1,244 a troy ounce, a record settlement
price on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange. Gold also hit an intraday record, surging
to $1,252.10. In euro terms, the metal also hit a record of 1,042.94 euros ($1,242.87) in the London market, a
gain of 2.5%.
The metal has surged over worries about Europe's debt woes and the slumping value of the euro. Investors in
metals and currency markets have been on alert for any sign that the world's central banks, and China in
particular, are shifting reserves out of the euro and into gold.
Though central banks typically are coy about investment decisions, there have been signs lately that they
might be shifting out of euros and into gold.
Russia's central bank, one of the world's largest holders of foreign-currency reserves, trimmed its currency
reserves by $6.6 billion in May, according to data on the bank's Web site, but increased its gold reserves by
$1.8 billion.
Last week, an Iranian news agency said the country had begun switching 45 billion euros of its
foreign-currency reserves into gold and dollars, a report that was unconfirmed.
"You won't see major players be blatant about increasing their gold exposure and reducing their euro
exposure, but it is a trend we've been witnessing in the past few months," said Kathy Lien, director of
currency research at GFT Forex in New York. "For the most part, whether they openly admit it or not, central
banks are increasingly worried about their exposure to euros."
Central banks would be joining a buying binge by individual investors who have piled into coins and
exchange-traded funds.
Central banks were net buyers of gold in 2009, according to the World Gold Council. India alone purchased
200 tons of gold from the International Monetary Fund. But appetite appeared to wane as gold traded at
record levels.
What has changed recently is that gold has shown its "relatively attractiveness" against other assets,
particularly for central banks in emerging markets, said Natalie Dempster, director of the World Gold Council.
Central banks and other large investors also are buying Treasury bonds and Swiss francs, other signs of an
effort to preserve capital, Ms. Lien said.
Some European investments, such as German bonds, are relatively secure, arguably safer havens than gold
or Treasurys. But the range of safe European options has dwindled as debt turmoil has spread, and the size
of the German bond market isn't large enough to accommodate the rising global demand for safe assets,
forcing central banks and large investors to look elsewhere, according to Alan Ruskin, global head of
currency strategy for RBS Securities in Stamford, Conn.
"This is a beginning, I am afraid," said Andy Smith, a senior metals strategist at Bache Commodities Group in
London. "Gold is reflecting so many things that could possibly go wrong."

Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Long 401(k) Menus Prove Hard to Digest
Investing in Funds: A Monthly Analysis --- Long 401(k) Menus Prove Hard to Digest
697 words
7 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C10
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Providers of 401(k) plans face a fundamental question these days: Is more more, or is more less?
In theory, it would seem employees are better off having a wide range of fund offerings in a plan. Funds
focused on alternative assets such as commodities, real estate, Treasury inflation-protected securities and
emerging-markets stocks can potentially balance risk and add value to a diversified portfolio inside a
retirement plan.
But for employers and 401(k) providers considering adding such offerings, "it's a really tricky issue, and the
pendulum has swung back and forth," says Gerry Mullane, principal and head of the institutional sales group
at Vanguard Group. "On the one hand, you want to give people a robust menu of choices, but you don't want
too many choices because it can cause choice overload, and people throw up their hands and quit."
In addition, some of these securities are very volatile. "It's a tricky call when you get into things like emerging
markets and commodities," says Russel Kinnel, director of mutual-fund research at Morningstar Inc. These
asset classes are "a great thing at 5%, but they're a horrible thing at 25%" of a portfolio. If employers are
going to make such funds available, they need to help people understand them, he says.
The typical defined-contribution plan has 18 funds that cover U.S. stocks of various sizes and styles, as well
as international stocks, cash, and stable-value funds, says David Wray, president of the Profit Sharing/401k
Council of America, or PSCA, a nonprofit association of plan sponsors. "The plans typically do not have more
specialty-type funds."
One exception is real estate, with 25% of plans offering some sort of real-estate fund, up from 15% five years
ago, says Mr. Wray. One reason for this, he says, is that real-estate investment trusts, or REITs, are easier
for employers and participants to understand, and are broad enough to provide some diversification.
"There's a sense that it is a more appropriate long-term investment compared to commodities, which by their
nature are highly volatile," he says.
While there hasn't been an influx of alternative-asset funds in employer-sponsored retirement plans, it doesn't
mean employers and 401(k) providers aren't considering adding them.
"Everyone's talking about China or risk for inflation, and that 'we need commodities in the plan,' " says
Gareth Lyons, an investment consultant with Morningstar Associates, a Morningstar unit that provides
investment-consulting services to plan sponsors and providers. "Even though those asset classes do give
good diversification benefits, if they aren't used properly, it's self-defeating," he says.
For that reason, Morningstar Associates recommends commodities funds as a supplement to more traditional
investments only if it thinks there are good options available and the plan sponsor is providing enough
education to plan participants.
And then there's the lesson of the late 1990s: "People wanted exposure to the Internet, the new-new thing,"
recalls Vanguard's Mr. Mullane. Some plans added technology funds, which had had outsized returns. "The
unfortunate occurrence was that participants bought high and sold low" when prices crashed.
"Tech requests have turned into requests for gold and commodities funds," says Mr. Lyons.
Some 401(k) investors can get access to less common asset classes in another way. According to PSCA,
about 15% of plans provide for a brokerage account, or "window," that lets investors hold almost any
securities they want, including individual stocks. This number is unchanged over the last five years.
"Many companies are very nervous about providing access to a brokerage account because they have to
ask: Is it in the interest of unsophisticated participants to have the availability to go out and invest in exotic
instruments?" says Mr. Wray.

--- Page 72 ---
Page 72 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, when the opportunity is offered, "it's typically used by more sophisticated, more well-educated
employees," he says. "It's unusual for the other employees to put their money in a brokerage account. Most
employees want a simple solution -- a brokerage account is not that."
---
Anna Prior

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Playing Gold Without Getting Killed
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Wealth Manager: Playing Gold Without Getting Killed
By Brett Arends
582 words
5 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
B9
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
You can see signs of a gold rush everywhere, from the nonstop TV commercials in the U.S. to the Emirates
Palace Hotel in Abu Dhabi, where a vending machine now dispenses gold coins.
If anything, the nine-year gold boom has intensified as traders nervous over the European financial crisis
have flocked to the metal's perceived safety. The assets in the SPDR Gold Trust (GLD), an exchange-traded
fund that tracks the price of gold, jumped by more than 10% in May alone.
The question for investors who have remained on the sidelines until now is whether there still is an
opportunity to join the stampede -- and, if so, how to do it without getting crushed.
Gold prices have risen nearly fivefold since 2001. Yet, remarkably, some analysts say the rally might still
have legs. John LaForge at Ned Davis Research notes that, adjusted for inflation, gold is little over half its
peak 1980 price. Charles de Vaulx, the value-minded manager of IVA Worldwide Fund, says gold's long rally
has merely put it in line with long-term averages in relation to the stock market.
Hedge-fund titans George Soros and John Paulson share the bullish view. They have big stakes in gold, and
have recently invested in smaller mining companies such as Kinross Gold Corp. and NovaGold Resources
Inc. in the hope these might jump higher than bigger miners if gold prices continue to rise.
But the long gold rally also is stoking fears of a bubble. While that would augur for more gains in the short
term, it also could set the stage for a crash later on. A similar pattern unfolded in the 1970s: gold rallied for
most of the decade, peaking in 1980 at around $850 an ounce. Then it plummeted, bottoming out at around
$250 in 2001.
The lesson: gold can be dangerous, and its steep rise only makes it more so. John Hydeskov, senior
currency analyst at Danske Bank in Copenhagen, warns that gold may be especially vulnerable to a
short-term pullback now. Too much speculative money has already chased the futures market higher, he
says.
That is why some investors are hoping to thread a needle, making bets they hope will pay off if prices go up,
while limiting their losses if the market drops.
One way to do that is to buy call options on the GLD exchange-traded fund. Call options give you the right to
buy the fund at a later date if prices rise further, while letting you walk away with only small losses if they
tank.
So instead of risking $119 a share on the GLD fund, investors could, for example, pay $16 for $120 call
options good until January 2012. That would give them the right to buy the shares at $120 if gold booms, and
limit their losses to $16 if gold tanks. (These are per-share values. Options trade in lots of 100.)
The call-option strategy "is a way of buying exposure cheaply," says Alan Lancz, a money manager for
institutions and wealthy clients in Toledo, Ohio. He says it lets investors use "a small amount of money to get
exposure even when prices are this high."
"It's one way to avoid losing incredible amounts of money," adds Larry Glazer, portfolio manager for
Mayflower Advisors in Boston. "At any given moment this thing could have a massive selloff."

As Global Recovery Sputters, Oil Falls by 4.2%, Copper by 4.3%

Oil, copper and other commodities tumbled Friday after a monthly U.S. unemployment report raised doubts

markets has increasingly been dominated by fears that sovereign debt will undermine European economies
and that China could seek to reduce its growth rate to cap inflation.

for the 2010 Census. Investors also received another euro-zone sovereign-debt scare after a leading official
in Hungary's ruling Fidesz party said the country's high debt levels could trigger a Greek-like fiscal crisis.

growth. July crude-oil futures fell $3.10, or 4.2%, to settle at $71.51 a barrel. Most-active July copper fell 4.3%

this past week, the largest one-week percentage decline since December 2008.
"The jobs data . . . were just a nail in the coffin," said Ira Epstein, director of the Ira Epstein division of Linn
Group.
Assets thought to hold value when the economic outlook turns sour saw big gains. Gold for August delivery,
seen as a haven, rose 0.6% to settle at $1,217.70 an ounce.
For oil and copper, Friday's losses erased some of the last gains made earlier in 2010, when the world
economy looked to be pulling out of the downturn faster than many anticipated.
Prices still may have further to fall, particularly in the oil market, which is entering its peak summer demand
season. Government demand data due Wednesday covering the Memorial Day holiday, when many
Americans take to the road, could set the tone for the rest of the summer, analysts said. "If demand comes in
weak, as I expect, we should see prices fall back more," said Greg Lawson, risk manager at Summit Energy.
Agricultural markets followed energy and stock markets lower, with most-active July soybeans settling 2.1%
lower at $9.35 a bushel and July corn ending down 2.7% at $3.40 a bushel.

Books: Five Best
WEEKEND JOURNAL
Books: Five Best
By Fouad Ajami
816 words
5 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
W8
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Fouad Ajami on his favorite books about financial dynasties]
1. Gold and Iron
By Fritz Stern
Knopf, 1977
In perhaps the single best book on financial history, Fritz Stern tells of the tangled relationship between Otto
von Bismarck and Gerson von Bleichroder. Statesman and banker, Junker and Jew, the two men rose
together against the background of a new German empire. The great themes of 19th-century German and
European history -- capitalism, high finance, Jewish emancipation and its nemesis -- intersect in this pair.
Bismarck needed money to finance the wars of German unification. That need gave Bleichroder his sway, but
he was never allowed to forget his Jewish origins; the ambiguity of Jewish success ran through Bleichroder's
life. His death came in 1893, Bismarck's five years later. Kristallnacht and the destruction of German Jews
were still decades away, but a reader can sense the terrible storm over the German horizon.
2. The House of Rothschild
By Niall Ferguson
Viking, two vols., 1998, 1999
"Money is the God of our time, and Rothschild is his prophet," the poet and essayist Heinrich Heine wrote in
1841. In Niall Ferguson, the House of Rothschild was to find its most ambitious and relentless historian.
Ferguson masterfully tells the story of the Rothschilds from the early years of the 19th century down to the
present. Five brothers, the sons of a man who was prohibited from owning property outside the Frankfurt
Jewish ghetto, emerged as Europe's most powerful bankers. The "Rothschild myth" depicted the five brothers
as the "metallic sovereigns" of Europe. An American populist, Coin Harvey, caught this dark view of the
House of Rothschild: They were "a vast black octopus stretching its tentacles around the world." The
Rothschilds survived many crises, Ferguson writes, thanks to an ethos of familial solidarity, a religiously
rooted morality and hard work.
3. Bankers and Pashas
By David Landes
Harvard, 1958
In a dazzling and beautifully written book, David Landes provides a memorable account of merchant
banking's push into Egypt in the 1860s. A hitherto impoverished land, Egypt came into sudden wealth when
the American Civil War wiped out what had been the world's main cotton supply, turning Egypt's "fuzzy white
fiber into gold." Alexandria boiled over, Landes tells us, as the highest and the lowest clambered into the
world of finance. A vainglorious ruler on the Nile, the Khedive Ismail Pasha, determined to make his country
into a piece of Europe, borrowed with abandon. He gave Egypt the Suez Canal (and commissioned Giuseppe
Verdi's "Aida"), but in a reign of just 13 years he also led the nation into bankruptcy and foreign dominion. The
cotton boom came and went, Alexandria turned unbearable again, and both the greedy foreign bankers and
the rapacious pashas were undone by their inordinate ambition.
4. Morgan
By Jean Strouse

--- Page 78 ---
Page 78 of 185 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Random House, 1999
Jean Strouse doesn't set out to redeem J.P. Morgan's reputation in writing his biography, but by the time she
is done he emerges as a figure of great probity. The U.S. didn't have a central bank until 1913, the year of
Morgan's death, for the man had been, in essence, the nation's central banker. He managed to keep America
on the gold standard during a monetary crisis in 1895, he saw the banking system through the crash of 1907.
He helped transform the U.S. from a largely agrarian society into an industrial colossus. In a legendary
exchange before a congressional committee in 1912, Morgan rejected the suggestion that commercial credit
was based primarily on money or property. "No sir; the first thing is character. Because a man I do not trust
could not get money from me on all the bonds in Christendom."
5. The Sixth Great Power
By Philip Ziegler
Knopf, 1988
In 1995, rogue trader Nick Leeson wiped out Barings PLC, bringing to an end a storied banking institution
with more than 200 years behind it. Philip Ziegler's "The Sixth Great Power" takes its title from Louis XVIII's
prime minister, Duc de Richelieu, who observed in 1818 that there were half a dozen great powers in Europe:
England, France, Prussia, Austria, Russia and Baring Brothers. The Barings rose in the late 18th century,
with Britain itself: The Industrial Revolution was in full stride, and London was the financial center of a
growing empire. The head of the Baring dynasty in the 1850s gave voice to a familial ambition: "Half my
pleasure is to work for a house which we intend to be perpetual; the king is dead, long live the king." Thomas
Baring couldn't have foreseen the tacky Nick Leeson, without pedigree or learning, taking down the hard work
of two centuries.
---
Mr. Ajami is a professor at Johns Hopkins and a senior fellow at the Hoover Institution.

IRAN
Report of Euro Sale
Puts Pressure on Currency
An Iranian news report that the country's central bank has begun switching 45 billion euros ($55 billion) of its
foreign-currency reserves into dollars and gold fueled fears that major holders of euros could abandon the
currency.
If true, the step would mark a reversal of President Mahmoud Ahmadinejad's order in September to shift more
of the nation's hard-currency reserves out of dollars and into euros. The Iranian central bank, which holds as
much as $97 billion in reserves, couldn't be reached to comment. The report was greeted with skepticism by
many in financial markets.
-- Katie Martin
---
BOSNIA-HERZEGOVINA
EU Assures Balkan States
On Potential Membership
The European Union pledged to keep its doors open to membership for the poorer states of the western
Balkans, despite mounting worries about expanding the group in the wake of the Greek financial crisis.
At a summit meeting in Sarajevo, the capital of Bosnia-Herzegovina, senior EU officials sought to reassure
government ministers from the Balkan states that once their nations meet the bloc's criteria for joining, they
will be admitted.
Still, serious hurdles remain.
Of the countries that emerged from the violent collapse of Yugoslavia beginning in the 1990s, only Slovenia
has succeeded in joining the EU.
Some diplomats fear the prospect of EU membership is losing its allure as an incentive for reconciliation and
improved national governance.
-- Gordon Fairclough
---
GENEVA
U.N. Report Criticizes
Secrecy of Drone Attacks
A special U.N. investigator on extrajudicial killings called for governments to be forthcoming about their
methods for killing terrorists and insurgents, especially if drones are involved. In a report to the U.N. Human
Rights Council, Philip Alston urged nations using drones to publish civilian casualty figures, lay out their rules
and safeguards for so-called targeted killings, and prove they have attempted to capture suspects without

Gold Rises on EU Worries; IMF Sale Has Little Impact
Commodities Report
Gold Rises on EU Worries; IMF Sale Has Little Impact
By Allen Sykora
403 words
2 June 2010
The Wall Street Journal
J
C13
English
Copyright 2010 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold posted its highest close in two weeks as investors sought the metal for protection against economic
uncertainty after a report said European banks face sizeable write-offs.
Gold for August delivery on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange rose $11.90, or 1%, to
$1,226.90 ounce, its loftiest settlement since May 17. Nearby June gold gained $12.60, or 1%, to $1,224.80.
Silver followed, with the July contract gaining 12.9 cents, or 0.7%, to $18.551 an ounce.
"You still have investors moving toward safe-haven assets like the dollar, yen, U.S. bonds and also gold,"
said Carlos Sanchez, associate director of research with CPM Group in New York. "There are ongoing
concerns over euro-zone debt problems on the minds of many investors."
The European Central Bank said Monday that euro-zone banks may have to write off loans totaling 195
billion euros ($240 billion) this year and next, adding to concerns that tighter credit markets could slow
economic growth on the continent. This comes on top of concerns about sovereign debt and the ability of
some European nations to reduce their deficits.
The European situation has many investors wanting to hold tangible assets such as gold, said Scott Meyers,
senior trading analyst in New York with the Pioneer Futures division of MF Global. This trend may continue
for the next few months, he said.
"There is going to be a conscious decision that investors have to make, whether it be interest rates or
precious metals," Mr. Meyers said. "I think the actual ability to own something is going to come into play, as
opposed to a portfolio."
The market largely shrugged off news that the International Monetary Fund sold an additional 14.4 metric
tons of gold in April. The IMF has been gradually selling some of its holdings as part of a plan to unload
403.3 metric tons to fund programs for poor nations.
Meanwhile, July platinum closed unchanged at $1,549.40 an ounce. September palladium fell $1.30, or 0.3%,
to $462.15.
A recent pullback in platinum prices is being used as a bargain-hunting opportunity by market participants
optimistic that a global economic recovery will continue, Mr. Sanchez said. The metal's main industrial use is
for auto catalytic converters.
